					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Japanese dish of vinegared rice and seafood</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Sushi_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Sushi (disambiguation)">Sushi (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Sushi-ya" redirects here. For the magazine originally known by this name, see <a href="/wiki/Neo_(magazine)" title="Neo (magazine)">Neo (magazine)</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Shushi" class="mw-redirect" title="Shushi">Shushi</a> or <a href="/wiki/Su_Shi" title="Su Shi">Su Shi</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>

img[src*="Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg"]{filter:invert(1)brightness(55%)contrast(250%)hue-rotate(180deg)}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .sister-inline-image img[src*="Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg"]{filter:invert(1)brightness(55%)contrast(250%)hue-rotate(180deg)}}</style><span class="noviewer sister-inline-image" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/20px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/40px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a></span> <a src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/20px-Commons-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="12" height="16" class="mw-file-element" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Sushi" class="extiw" title="commons:Sushi">Media&#58; Sushi</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Sushi</b><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">すし, 寿司, 鮨, 鮓</span></span>; <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1177148991">.mw-parser-output .IPA-label-small{font-size:85%}.mw-parser-output .references .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .infobox .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .navbox .IPA-label-small{font-size:100%}</style><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">pronounced</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="ja-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Japanese" title="Help:IPA/Japanese">&#91;sɯɕiꜜ&#93;</a></span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1177148991" /><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">or</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="ja-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Japanese" title="Help:IPA/Japanese">&#91;sɯꜜɕi&#93;</a></span> <span class="ext-phonos"><span data-nosnippet="" id="ooui-php-1" class="noexcerpt ext-phonos-PhonosButton ext-phonos-PhonosButton-emptylabel oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-buttonElement oo-ui-buttonElement-frameless oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-buttonWidget" data-ooui="{&quot;_&quot;:&quot;mw.Phonos.PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;href&quot;:&quot;\/\/upload.wikimedia.org\/wikipedia\/commons\/transcoded\/6\/69\/TomJ-Sushi.ogg\/TomJ-Sushi.ogg.mp3&quot;,&quot;rel&quot;:[&quot;nofollow&quot;],&quot;framed&quot;:false,&quot;icon&quot;:&quot;volumeUp&quot;,&quot;data&quot;:{&quot;ipa&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;lang&quot;:&quot;en&quot;,&quot;wikibase&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;file&quot;:&quot;TomJ-Sushi.ogg&quot;},&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;noexcerpt&quot;,&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton-emptylabel&quot;]}"><a role="button" tabindex="0" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/69/TomJ-Sushi.ogg/TomJ-Sushi.ogg.mp3" rel="nofollow" aria-label="Play audio" title="Play audio" class="oo-ui-buttonElement-button"><span class="oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-volumeUp"></span><span class="oo-ui-labelElement-label"></span><span class="oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicatorElement-noIndicator"></span></a></span><sup class="ext-phonos-attribution noexcerpt navigation-not-searchable"><a href="/wiki/File:TomJ-Sushi.ogg" title="File:TomJ-Sushi.ogg">ⓘ</a></sup></span>)</span> is a traditional <a href="/wiki/Japanese_dish" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese dish">Japanese dish</a> made with vinegared <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">鮨飯</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">sushi-meshi</i></span><span style="margin-left:.09em">)</span></span>, typically seasoned with <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a>, and combined with a variety of ingredients<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ねた</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">neta</i></span>)</span>, such as <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vegetables" class="mw-redirect" title="Vegetables">vegetables</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a>; raw seafood is the most common, although some may be cooked. While sushi has numerous styles and presentations, the current defining component is the vinegared rice, also known as <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">shari</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">しゃり</span></span>)</span>, or <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">sumeshi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">酢飯</span></span>)</span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The modern form of sushi is believed to have been created by <a href="/wiki/Hanaya_Yohei" title="Hanaya Yohei">Hanaya Yohei</a>, who invented <i>nigiri-zushi</i>, the most commonly recognized type today, in which seafood is placed on hand-pressed, vinegared rice. This innovation occurred around 1824 in the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> (1603–1867). It was the fast food of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dnin" title="Chōnin">chōnin</a></i> class in the Edo period.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Sushi is traditionally made with medium-grain <a href="/wiki/White_rice" title="White rice">white rice</a>, although it can also be prepared with <a href="/wiki/Brown_rice" title="Brown rice">brown rice</a> or short-grain rice. It is commonly prepared with seafood, such as <a href="/wiki/Squid_as_food" title="Squid as food">squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eel_as_food" title="Eel as food">eel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_amberjack" title="Japanese amberjack">yellowtail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salmon_as_food" title="Salmon as food">salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuna_as_food" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuna as food">tuna</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Crab_stick" title="Crab stick">imitation crab meat</a> (<i>surimi</i>). Certain types of sushi are vegetarian. It is often served with <a href="/wiki/Gari_(ginger)" title="Gari (ginger)">pickled ginger</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">gari</i></span><span style="margin-left:.09em">)</span></span>, <a href="/wiki/Wasabi" title="Wasabi">wasabi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>. <a href="/wiki/Daikon_radish" class="mw-redirect" title="Daikon radish">Daikon radish</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pickled_daikon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pickled daikon">pickled daikon</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">takuan</i></span>)</span> are popular garnishes for the dish.
</p><p>Sushi is sometimes confused with <a href="/wiki/Sashimi" title="Sashimi">sashimi</a>, a dish that consists of thinly sliced raw <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> or occasionally meat, without sushi rice.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>Today, sushi is considered one of the most popular dishes in the world.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The word <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">sushi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">寿司</span></span>; すし)</span> is from Japanese Kanji <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">鮨</span></span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">寿司</span></span> is <a href="/wiki/Ateji" title="Ateji">ateji</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_sushi" title="History of sushi">History of sushi</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Narezushi"><i>Narezushi</i></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Narezushi"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Barack_Obama_and_Shinzo_Abe_at_Sukyabashi_Jiro_April_2014.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Barack_Obama_and_Shinzo_Abe_at_Sukyabashi_Jiro_April_2014.jpg/250px-Barack_Obama_and_Shinzo_Abe_at_Sukyabashi_Jiro_April_2014.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Barack_Obama_and_Shinzo_Abe_at_Sukyabashi_Jiro_April_2014.jpg/500px-Barack_Obama_and_Shinzo_Abe_at_Sukyabashi_Jiro_April_2014.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1000" /></a><figcaption>President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> and Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Shinzo_Abe" title="Shinzo Abe">Shinzo Abe</a> at <a href="/wiki/Sukiyabashi_Jiro" title="Sukiyabashi Jiro">Sukiyabashi Jiro</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>A dish known as <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">narezushi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">馴れ寿司, 熟寿司</span></span>)</span>, "matured fish", stored in fermented rice for possibly months at a time, has been cited as one of the early influences for the Japanese practice of applying rice on raw fish. The fish was fermented with <a href="/wiki/Rice_vinegar" title="Rice vinegar">rice vinegar</a>, salt, and rice, after which the rice was discarded.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-worldcat1993&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldcat1993-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Narezushi</i></span> is also called <b><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">honnare</i></span></b>, meaning "fully fermented", as opposed to <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">namanare</i></span>, meaning "partially fermented", a type of sushi that appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Muromachi_period" title="Muromachi period">Muromachi period</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jst140223&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jst140223-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Fermented fish using rice, such as <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">narezushi</i></span>, originated in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, where it was made to preserve <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_fish" title="Freshwater fish">freshwater fish</a>, possibly in the <a href="/wiki/Mekong_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Mekong River">Mekong River</a> basin, which is now <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/Irrawaddy_River" title="Irrawaddy River">Irrawaddy River</a> basin, which is now <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jst140223&#95;11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jst140223-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first mention of a <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">narezushi</i></span>-like food is in a Chinese dictionary thought to be from the fourth century, in this instance referring to salted fish that had been placed in cooked or steamed rice, which caused it to undergo a fermentation process via <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-sanchez2008&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sanchez2008-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fermentation methods following similar logic in other Asian rice cultures include <span title="Filipino-language text"><i lang="fil"><a href="/wiki/Burong_isda" title="Burong isda">burong isda</a></i></span>, <span title="Filipino-language text"><i lang="fil"><a href="/wiki/Balao-balao" title="Balao-balao">balao-balao</a></i></span>, and <span title="Maguindanaon-language text"><i lang="mdh"><a href="/wiki/Tinapayan" title="Tinapayan">tinapayan</a></i></span> of the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>; <i><a href="/wiki/Pekasam" title="Pekasam">pekasam</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>; <i><a href="/wiki/Padaek" title="Padaek">padaek</a></i> (<span title="Lao-language text"><span lang="lo">ປາແດກ</span></span>) of Laos; <span title="Thai-language romanization"><i lang="th-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Pla_ra" title="Pla ra">pla ra</a></i></span> (<span title="Thai-language text"><span lang="th">ปลาร้า</span></span>) of Thailand; <i><a href="/wiki/Sikhae" title="Sikhae">sikhae</a></i> (<span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">식해</span></span>) of <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>; and <i>mắm</i> of Vietnam.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-worldcat1993&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldcat1993-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-The&#95;Guardian,&#95;UK-&#95;&#39;Chopsticks&#95;at&#95;dawn&#95;for&#95;a&#95;sushi&#95;showdown&#39;&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian,_UK-_&#39;Chopsticks_at_dawn_for_a_sushi_showdown&#39;-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Lacto-fermentation" class="mw-redirect" title="Lacto-fermentation">lacto-fermentation</a> of the rice prevents the fish from spoiling. When wet-field rice cultivation was introduced during the <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi period</a>, lakes and rivers would flood during the rainy season and fish would get caught in the rice paddy fields. Pickling was a way to preserve the excess fish and guarantee food for the following months, and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">narezushi</i></span> became an important source of protein for Japanese consumers. The term <i>sushi</i> literally means "sour-tasting", as the overall dish has a sour and <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a> or savory taste. The term comes from an <a href="/wiki/Japanese_adjectives#Archaic_forms" title="Japanese adjectives">antiquated</a> <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">し</span></span> <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">shi</i></span> terminal-form conjugation, no longer used in other contexts, of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_adjectives" title="Japanese adjectives">adjectival verb</a> <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">sui</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">酸い</span></span>, "to be sour")</span>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> resulting in the term <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">sushi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">酸し</span></span>)</span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Narezushi</i></span> still exists as a regional specialty, notably as <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">funa-zushi</i></span> from <a href="/wiki/Shiga_Prefecture" title="Shiga Prefecture">Shiga Prefecture</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tsukiji&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tsukiji-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Y%C5%8Dr%C5%8D_Code" title="Yōrō Code">Yōrō Code</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">養老律令</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Yōrō-ritsuryō</i></span>)</span> of 718, the characters for "<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">鮨</span></span>" and "<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">鮓</span></span>" are written as a tribute to the Japanese imperial court, and although there are various theories as to what exactly this food was, it is possible that it referred to <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">narezushi</i></span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Namanare"><i>Namanare</i></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Namanare"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Until the early 19th century, sushi slowly changed with Japanese cuisine. The Japanese started eating three meals a day, rice was boiled instead of steamed, and of large importance was the development of rice vinegar.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moritzen&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moritzen-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>During the Muromachi period (1336–1573), the Japanese invented a style of sushi called <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">namanare</i></span> or <b><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">namanari</i></span></b> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">生成, なまなれ, なまなり</span></span>), which means "partially fermented". The fermentation period of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">namanare</i></span> was shorter than that of the earlier <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">narezushi</i></span>, and the rice used for fermentation was also eaten with the fish. In other words, with the invention of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">namanare</i></span>, sushi changed from a preserved fish food to a food where fish and rice are eaten together. After the appearance of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">namanare</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">sake</a> and sake lees were used to shorten fermentation, and vinegar was used in the Edo period.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jst140223&#95;11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jst140223-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Hayazushi"><i>Hayazushi</i></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hayazushi"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hiroshige_Bowl_of_Sushi.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Hiroshige_Bowl_of_Sushi.jpg/250px-Hiroshige_Bowl_of_Sushi.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="197" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Hiroshige_Bowl_of_Sushi.jpg/500px-Hiroshige_Bowl_of_Sushi.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2962" data-file-height="2329" /></a><figcaption><i>Bowl of Sushi</i> by <a href="/wiki/Hiroshige" title="Hiroshige">Hiroshige</a> (1797–1858). <i><a href="#Makizushi">Makizushi</a></i> with rice rolled in <i><a href="/wiki/Tamagoyaki" title="Tamagoyaki">tamagoyaki</a></i> (front) and <i><a href="#Nigirizushi">nigirizushi</a></i> with shrimp (back).</figcaption></figure>
<p>During the Edo period (1603–1867), a third type of sushi, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">haya-zushi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">早寿司, 早ずし</span></span>, "fast sushi")</span>, was developed. <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Haya-zushi</i></span> differed from earlier sushi in that instead of lactic fermentation of rice, vinegar, a fermented food, was mixed with rice to give it a sour taste so that it could be eaten at the same time as the fish. Previously, sushi had evolved with a focus on shortening the fermentation period, but with the invention of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">haya-zushi</i></span>, which is simply mixed with vinegar, the fermentation process was eliminated and sushi became a fast food. Many types of sushi known in the world today, such as <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="#Chirashizushi">chirashizushi</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">散らし寿司</span></span>, "scattered sushi")</span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="#Inarizushi">inarizushi</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">稲荷寿司</span></span>, "<a href="/wiki/Inari_%C5%8Ckami" title="Inari Ōkami">Inari</a> sushi")</span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="#Makizushi">makizushi</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">巻寿司</span></span>, "rolled sushi")</span>, and <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="#Nigirizushi">nigirizushi</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">握り寿司</span></span>, "hand-pressed sushi")</span>, were invented during this period, and they are a type of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">haya-zushi</i></span>. Each region utilizes local flavors to produce a variety of sushi that has been passed down for many generations. A 1689 cookbook describes <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">haya-zushi</i></span>, and a 1728 cookbook describes pouring vinegar over <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="#Oshizushi">hako-zushi</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">箱ずし</span></span>, "box sushi")</span> (square sushi made by filling a wooden frame with rice).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jst140223&#95;11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jst140223-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Today's style of <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">nigirizushi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">握り寿司</span></span>, "hand-pressed sushi")</span>, consisting of an oblong mound of rice with a slice of fish draped over it, became popular in <a href="/wiki/Edo" title="Edo">Edo</a> (contemporary Tokyo) in the 1820s or 1830s. One common story of the origin of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">nigirizushi</i></span> is of the chef Hanaya Yohei (1799–1858), who invented or perfected the technique in 1824 at his shop in <a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%8Dgoku" title="Ryōgoku">Ryōgoku</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tsukiji&#95;19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tsukiji-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">nigirizushi</i></span> of this period was somewhat different from modern <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">nigirizushi</i></span>. The sushi rice of this period was about three times the size of today's <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">nigirizushi</i></span>. The amount of vinegar used was half that of today's sushi, and the type of vinegar developed during this period, called <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">aka-su</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">赤酢</span></span>, "red vinegar")</span>, was made by fermenting sake lees. They also used slightly more salt than in modern times instead of sugar. Seafood served over rice was prepared in a variety of ways. This red vinegar was developed by Nakano Matazaemon (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">中野 又佐衛門</span></span>), who is the founder of <a href="/wiki/Mizkan" title="Mizkan">Mizkan</a>, a company that still develops and sells vinegar and other seasonings today.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jst140223&#95;11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jst140223-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The dish was originally termed <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Edomae zushi</i></span> as it used freshly caught fish from the <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Edo-mae</i></span> (Edo or <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Bay" title="Tokyo Bay">Tokyo Bay</a>); the term <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Edomae nigirizushi</i></span> is still used today as a by-word for quality sushi, regardless of its ingredients' origins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Conveyor_belt_sushi">Conveyor belt sushi</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Conveyor belt sushi"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_090813-N-0413R-250_Sailors_and_their_families_enjoy_sushi_while_visiting_the_historic_city_of_Kamakura,_Japan_during_orientation.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/US_Navy_090813-N-0413R-250_Sailors_and_their_families_enjoy_sushi_while_visiting_the_historic_city_of_Kamakura%2C_Japan_during_orientation.jpg/250px-US_Navy_090813-N-0413R-250_Sailors_and_their_families_enjoy_sushi_while_visiting_the_historic_city_of_Kamakura%2C_Japan_during_orientation.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="178" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/US_Navy_090813-N-0413R-250_Sailors_and_their_families_enjoy_sushi_while_visiting_the_historic_city_of_Kamakura%2C_Japan_during_orientation.jpg/500px-US_Navy_090813-N-0413R-250_Sailors_and_their_families_enjoy_sushi_while_visiting_the_historic_city_of_Kamakura%2C_Japan_during_orientation.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3708" data-file-height="2646" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Conveyor_belt_sushi" title="Conveyor belt sushi">Conveyor belt sushi</a> restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Kamakura" title="Kamakura">Kamakura</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1958, Yoshiaki Shiraishi opened the first <a href="/wiki/Conveyor_belt_sushi" title="Conveyor belt sushi">conveyor belt sushi</a> restaurant<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">回転寿司</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">kaiten-zushi</i></span><span style="margin-left:.09em">)</span></span> named "<a href="/wiki/Genroku_culture" title="Genroku culture">Genroku</a> Zushi" in <a href="/wiki/Higashi-Osaka" class="mw-redirect" title="Higashi-Osaka">Higashi-Osaka</a>. Conveyor belt sushi is a casual and affordable type of sushi restaurant <sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>. 
</p><p>Conveyor belts are typically installed along tables or counters in the restaurant and are used to carry plates of sushi past diners, who can choose to take whatever they want <sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>. Generally, the bill is based on the number of sushi plates, with different colors representing a particular price.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-magnier&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-magnier-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-walker14224&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walker14224-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-genroku11223&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-genroku11223-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>When Genroku Sushi opened a restaurant at the <a href="/wiki/Expo_%2770" title="Expo &#39;70">Japan World Exposition, Osaka, 1970</a>, it won an award at the expo, and conveyor belt sushi restaurants became known throughout Japan. In 1973, an automatic tea dispenser was developed, which is now used in conveyor belt sushi restaurants today. When the patent for conveyor belt sushi restaurants expired, a chain of conveyor belt sushi restaurants was established, spreading conveyor belt sushi throughout <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and further popularizing and lowering the price of sushi. By 2021, the conveyor belt sushi market had grown to 700 billion yen and spread outside Japan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-magnier&#95;26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-magnier-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-walker14224&#95;27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walker14224-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-genroku11223&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-genroku11223-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sushi_in_English">Sushi in English</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Sushi in English"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The earliest written mention of <i>sushi</i> in English described in the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> is in an 1893 book, <i>A Japanese Interior</i>, where it mentions sushi as "a roll of cold rice with fish, sea-weed, or some other flavoring".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is an earlier mention of sushi in <a href="/wiki/James_Curtis_Hepburn" title="James Curtis Hepburn">James Hepburn</a>'s Japanese–English dictionary from 1873,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and an 1879 article on Japanese cookery in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Notes_and_Queries" title="Notes and Queries">Notes and Queries</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite common misconception among English speakers, sushi does not mean "raw seafood."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Types">Types</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Types"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Typical_japanese_sushi_set.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Typical_japanese_sushi_set.jpg/250px-Typical_japanese_sushi_set.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="129" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Typical_japanese_sushi_set.jpg/500px-Typical_japanese_sushi_set.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1240" /></a><figcaption>Sushi platter to go</figcaption></figure>
<p>The common ingredient in all types of sushi is vinegared <a href="#sushi_rice">sushi rice</a>. Fillings, toppings, condiments, and preparation <a href="/wiki/List_of_sushi_and_sashimi_ingredients" title="List of sushi and sashimi ingredients">vary widely</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Due to <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Rendaku" title="Rendaku">rendaku</a></i></span> <a href="/wiki/Consonant_mutation" title="Consonant mutation">consonant mutation</a>, <i>sushi</i> is pronounced with <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">zu</i></span> instead of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">su</i></span> when a prefix is attached, as in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">nigirizushi</i></span>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Chirashizushi"><i>Chirashizushi</i></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Chirashizushi"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Chirashi-zushi.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Chirashi-zushi.jpg/250px-Chirashi-zushi.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Chirashi-zushi.jpg/500px-Chirashi-zushi.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a><figcaption><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Chirashizushi</i></span> with raw ingredients</figcaption></figure>
<p><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Chirashizushi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ちらし寿司</span></span>, "scattered sushi"; also referred to as <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">barazushi</i></span>)</span> serves the rice in a bowl and tops it with a variety of raw fish and vegetable garnishes. It is popular because it is filling, fast, and easy to make.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is eaten annually on <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Hinamatsuri" title="Hinamatsuri">Hinamatsuri</a></i></span> in March and <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Day_(Japan)" title="Children&#39;s Day (Japan)">Children's Day</a> in May.
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Edomae chirashizushi</i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Edo" title="Edo">Edo</a>-style scattered sushi) is served with uncooked ingredients in an artful arrangement.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Gomokuzushi</i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Kansai_region" title="Kansai region">Kansai</a>-style sushi) consists of cooked or uncooked ingredients mixed in the body of rice.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Sake-zushi</i></span> (Kyushu-style sushi) uses rice wine over vinegar in preparing the rice and is topped with <a href="/wiki/Shrimp_and_prawn_as_food" title="Shrimp and prawn as food">shrimp</a>, sea bream, <a href="/wiki/Octopus_as_food" title="Octopus as food">octopus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shiitake" title="Shiitake">shiitake mushrooms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bamboo_shoot" title="Bamboo shoot">bamboo shoots</a>, and shredded omelette.</li></ul>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Inarizushi"><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Inarizushi</i></span></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Inarizushi"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Kantofu_Inarizushi.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Kantofu_Inarizushi.jpg/250px-Kantofu_Inarizushi.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Kantofu_Inarizushi.jpg/500px-Kantofu_Inarizushi.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3216" data-file-height="2136" /></a><figcaption>Three pieces of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">inarizushi</i></span></figcaption></figure>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1314755338">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:var(--background-color-interactive-subtle,#f8f9fa);color:inherit;display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .infobox .side-box{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1;min-width:0}}@media(min-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1311551236">@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .sistersitebox{display:none!important}}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .sistersitebox img[src*="Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg"]{filter:invert(1)brightness(55%)contrast(250%)hue-rotate(180deg)}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .sistersitebox img[src*="Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg"]{filter:invert(1)brightness(55%)contrast(250%)hue-rotate(180deg)}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409" />
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/40px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/60px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/120px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">Wikibooks <a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Cookbook">Cookbook</a> has a recipe/module on
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409" /><div class="plainlist" style="margin-left: 1.6em;">
<ul><li><i><b><a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook:Inarizushi" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Cookbook:Inarizushi"> Inarizushi</a></b></i></li></ul>
</div></div></div>
</div>
<p><i>Inarizushi</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">稲荷寿司</span></span>)</span> is a pouch of <a href="/wiki/Abura-age" title="Abura-age">fried tofu</a> typically filled with sushi rice alone. According to Shinto lore, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">inarizushi</i></span> is named after the god <a href="/wiki/Inari_%C5%8Ckami" title="Inari Ōkami">Inari</a>. Foxes, messengers of Inari, are believed to have a fondness for fried <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a> and in some regions an Inari-zushi roll has pointed corners that resemble fox ears, thus reinforcing the association.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The shape of Inarizushi varies by region. Inarizushi usually has a rectangular shape in <a href="/wiki/Kant%C5%8D_region" title="Kantō region">Kantō region</a> and a triangle shape in the Kansai region.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Regional variations include pouches made from a thin omelette (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">帛紗寿司</span></span>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">fukusa-zushi</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">茶巾寿司</span></span>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">chakin-zushi</i></span>), instead of fried tofu. It should not be confused with <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">inari maki</i></span>, a sushi roll filled with seasoned fried tofu.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Cone sushi is a variant of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">inarizushi</i></span> originating in <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Hawaii" title="Cuisine of Hawaii">Hawaii</a> that may include <a href="/wiki/Green_bean" title="Green bean">green beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carrot" title="Carrot">carrots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctium_lappa" title="Arctium lappa">gobo</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Poke_(Hawaiian_dish)" class="mw-redirect" title="Poke (Hawaiian dish)">poke</a> along with rice, wrapped in a triangular <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Abura-age" title="Abura-age">abura-age</a></i></span> piece. It is often sold in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">okazu-ya</i></span> (Japanese <a href="/wiki/Delicatessen" title="Delicatessen">delis</a>) and as a component of <a href="/wiki/Bento" title="Bento">bento</a> boxes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Makizushi"><i>Makizushi</i></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Makizushi"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Makizushi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">巻き寿司</span></span>, "rolled sushi")</span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Norimaki" title="Norimaki">norimaki</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">海苔巻き</span></span>, "<a href="/wiki/Nori" title="Nori">nori</a> roll"; used generically for other dishes as well)</span> or <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">makimono</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">巻物</span></span>, "variety of rolls")</span> is a cylindrical piece formed with the help of a mat known as a <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Makisu" title="Makisu">makisu</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">巻き簾</span></span>)</span>. <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Makizushi</i></span> is generally wrapped in nori (seaweed) but is occasionally wrapped in a thin <a href="/wiki/Omelette" title="Omelette">omelette</a>, soy paper, <a href="/wiki/Cucumber" title="Cucumber">cucumber</a>, or <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shiso" title="Shiso">shiso</a></i></span> (perilla) leaves. <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Makizushi</i></span> is often cut into six or eight pieces, constituting a single roll order. Short-grain white rice is usually used, although short-grain brown rice, like <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">olive oil</a> on nori, is now becoming more widespread among the health-conscious. Rarely, sweet rice is mixed in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makizushi</i></span> rice.
</p><p>Nowadays, the rice in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makizushi</i></span> can be many kinds of <a href="/wiki/Black_rice" title="Black rice">black rice</a>, boiled rice, and cereals. Besides the common ingredients listed above, some varieties may include cheese, spicy cooked squid, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Yakiniku" title="Yakiniku">yakiniku</a></i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kamaboko" title="Kamaboko">kamaboko</a></i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Lunch_meat" title="Lunch meat">lunch meat</a>, sausage, bacon or <a href="/wiki/Spicy_tuna_roll" title="Spicy tuna roll">spicy tuna</a>. The nori may be brushed with <a href="/wiki/Sesame_oil" title="Sesame oil">sesame oil</a> or sprinkled with <a href="/wiki/Sesame" title="Sesame">sesame seeds</a>. In a variation, sliced pieces of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makizushi</i></span> may be lightly fried with <a href="/wiki/Eggs_as_food" title="Eggs as food">egg</a> coating.
</p><p>Below are some common types of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makizushi</i></span>, but many other kinds exist.
</p>
<ul><li><span class="anchor" id="futomaki"></span><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Futomaki</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">太巻</span></span>, "thick, large, or fat rolls")</span> is a large, cylindrical style of sushi, usually with nori on the outside.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-omae&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-omae-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A typical <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">futomaki</i></span> is <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>five to six centimeters (2 to <span class="frac">2<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>&#160;in) in diameter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Strada&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strada-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They are often made with two, three, or more fillings that are chosen for their complementary tastes and colors. <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Futomaki</i></span> are often vegetarian, and may use strips of cucumber, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kanpy%C5%8D_(food)" title="Kanpyō (food)">kampyō</a></i></span> gourd, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">takenoko</i></span> (bamboo shoots), or <a href="/wiki/Nelumbo_nucifera" title="Nelumbo nucifera">lotus root</a>. Strips of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Tamagoyaki" title="Tamagoyaki">tamagoyaki</a></i></span> omelette, tiny fish <a href="/wiki/Roe" title="Roe">roe</a>, chopped tuna, and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">oboro</i></span> <a href="/wiki/Whitefish_(fisheries_term)" title="Whitefish (fisheries term)">whitefish</a> flakes are typical non-vegetarian fillings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-omae&#95;42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-omae-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Traditionally, the vinegared rice is lightly seasoned with salt and sugar. Popular proteins are fish cakes, imitation crab meat, egg, tuna, or shrimp. Vegetables usually include cucumber, lettuce, and <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Takuan" title="Takuan">takuan</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">沢庵</span></span>, pickled radish)</span>.</li>
<li><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Tamago makizushi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">玉子巻き寿司</span></span>)</span> is <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makizushi</i></span> is wrapped in a thin omelet.</li>
<li><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Tempura makizushi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">天ぷら 巻き寿司</span></span>)</span> or <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">agezushi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">揚げ寿司ロール</span></span>)</span> is a fried version of the dish.</li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Ehomaki"></span>During the evening of the festival of <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Setsubun" title="Setsubun">Setsubun</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%AF%80%E5%88%86" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:節分">節分</a></span></span>)</span>, it is traditional in the Kansai region to eat a particular kind of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">futomaki</i></span> in its uncut cylindrical form, called <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">ehōmaki</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">惠方巻</span></span>, "lucky direction roll")</span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 2000 the custom had spread to all of Japan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dijitaru&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dijitaru-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Ehōmaki</i></span> is a roll composed of seven ingredients considered to be lucky. The typical ingredients include <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kanpyō</i></span>, egg, eel, and shiitake mushroom. <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Ehōmaki</i></span> often include other ingredients too. People usually eat the <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">ehōmaki</i></span> while facing the direction considered to be auspicious that year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Hosomaki"></span><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Hosomaki</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">細巻</span></span>, "thin rolls")</span> is a type of small cylindrical sushi with nori on the outside. A typical <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">hosomaki</i></span> has a diameter of about 2.5 centimeters (1&#160;in).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Strada&#95;43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strada-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They generally contain only one filling, often tuna, cucumber, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kanpyō</i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Natt%C5%8D" title="Nattō">nattō</a></i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Umeboshi" title="Umeboshi">umeboshi</a></i></span> paste, and squid with <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shiso" title="Shiso">shiso</a></i></span> (Japanese herb).
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Kappamaki" title="Kappamaki">Kappamaki</a></i> (河童巻) is a kind of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">hosomaki</i></span> filled with cucumber. It is named after the Japanese legendary water imp, fond of cucumbers, called the <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kappa_(folklore)" title="Kappa (folklore)">kappa</a></i></span>. Traditionally, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kappamaki</i></span> is consumed to clear the palate between eating raw fish and other kinds of food so that the flavors of the fish are distinct from the tastes of other foods.</li>
<li><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Tekkamaki</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">鉄火巻</span></span>)</span> is a kind of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">hosomaki</i></span> filled with raw tuna. Although it is believed that the word <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">tekka</i></span>, meaning "red hot iron", alludes to the color of the tuna flesh or salmon flesh, it actually originated as a quick snack to eat in gambling dens called <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">tekkaba</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">鉄火場</span></span>)</span>, much like the origins of the <a href="/wiki/Sandwich" title="Sandwich">sandwich</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Negitoromaki</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ねぎとろ巻</span></span>)</span> is a kind of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">hosomaki</i></span> filled with <a href="/wiki/Negitoro" title="Negitoro">negitoro</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Scallion" title="Scallion">scallion</a> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">negi</i></span>) and chopped tuna (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Toro_(sushi)" title="Toro (sushi)">toro</a></i></span>). Fatty tuna is often used in this style.</li>
<li><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Tsunamayomaki</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ツナマヨ巻</span></span>)</span> is a kind of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">hosomaki</i></span> filled with canned tuna tossed with <a href="/wiki/Mayonnaise" title="Mayonnaise">mayonnaise</a>.</li></ul></li>
<li><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Temaki</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">手巻</span></span>, "hand roll")</span> is a large cone-shaped style of sushi with nori on the outside and the ingredients spilling out the wide end. A typical <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">temaki</i></span> is about 10 centimeters (4&#160;in) long and is eaten with the fingers because it is too awkward to pick it up with <a href="/wiki/Chopsticks" title="Chopsticks">chopsticks</a>. For optimal taste and texture, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">temaki</i></span> must be eaten quickly after being made because the nori cone soon absorbs moisture from the filling and loses its crispness, making it somewhat difficult to bite through. For this reason, the nori in pre-made or take-out temaki is sealed in plastic film, which is removed immediately before eating.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
