2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">Australian sushi from a restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>. The fillings from top to bottom are tobiko, fried chicken and California.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Today_Special_California_Roll,_Spicy_Tuna_Sushi_-_Diane_Sushi_AUD1.60,_AUD1.80_-_k850i_(4330232606).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Today_Special_California_Roll%2C_Spicy_Tuna_Sushi_-_Diane_Sushi_AUD1.60%2C_AUD1.80_-_k850i_%284330232606%29.jpg/250px-Today_Special_California_Roll%2C_Spicy_Tuna_Sushi_-_Diane_Sushi_AUD1.60%2C_AUD1.80_-_k850i_%284330232606%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Today_Special_California_Roll%2C_Spicy_Tuna_Sushi_-_Diane_Sushi_AUD1.60%2C_AUD1.80_-_k850i_%284330232606%29.jpg/330px-Today_Special_California_Roll%2C_Spicy_Tuna_Sushi_-_Diane_Sushi_AUD1.60%2C_AUD1.80_-_k850i_%284330232606%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Today_Special_California_Roll%2C_Spicy_Tuna_Sushi_-_Diane_Sushi_AUD1.60%2C_AUD1.80_-_k850i_%284330232606%29.jpg/500px-Today_Special_California_Roll%2C_Spicy_Tuna_Sushi_-_Diane_Sushi_AUD1.60%2C_AUD1.80_-_k850i_%284330232606%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">Australian California roll and tuna roll, served in a bag as a takeaway snack.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Australian sushi is a thick hand roll made from half a standard sheet of nori. It is similar to <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="#Makizushi">futomaki</a></i></span> thick rolls but is often served uncut as an on-the-go snack.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AusSushi&#95;101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AusSushi-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Typical fillings in Australian sushi include teriyaki chicken, salmon and avocado, tuna, and prawn.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Australian California rolls are very different from American California rolls, with the nori wrapping the rice and fillings always on the outside, and no <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Tobiko" title="Tobiko">tobiko</a></i></span> nor cream cheese. 
</p><p>It is widely available in affordable takeaway joints in Australia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sushi in Japanese restaurants has existed in Australia since the 1950s, but the first Australian-style sushi only appeared in 1995, in a stall called Sushi-Jin in the Target Centre food court at 246 <a href="/wiki/Bourke_Street" title="Bourke Street">Bourke Street</a>, Melbourne. The owner, Toshihiro Shindo, started selling takeaway sushi rolls which he adapted to Australian tastes. The store closed in 2008.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of 2024, Japanese cuisine is the most popular cuisine in Australia with sushi as the third overall most popular food item, after hot dogs and pizza.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Canada">Canada</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Canada"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Vancouver_sushi_pieces_dllu.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Vancouver_sushi_pieces_dllu.jpg/250px-Vancouver_sushi_pieces_dllu.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="242" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Vancouver_sushi_pieces_dllu.jpg/500px-Vancouver_sushi_pieces_dllu.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="7760" data-file-height="7501" /></a><figcaption>Some examples of Canadian sushi, including <i>aburi oshizushi</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">炙り押し寿司</span></span>)</span>, which was invented in Vancouver, and sushi donut.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Many of the styles seen in the United States are also seen in Canada and their own. Doshi (a portmanteau of <i>donut</i> and <i>sushi</i>) is a donut-shaped rice ball on a deep-fried crab or imitation crab cake topped with sushi ingredients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Maki <a href="/wiki/Poutine" title="Poutine">poutine</a> is similar to <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makizushi</i></span> in style except it is topped with cheese curds and gravy and contains duck <a href="/wiki/Confit" title="Confit">confit</a>, more cheese curds, and sweet potato tempura.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sushi cake is made of crab meat, avocado, shiitake mushroom, salmon, spicy tuna, and tobiko and served on sushi rice, then torched with spicy mayonnaise, barbecue sauce, and balsamic reduction, and dotted with caper and garlic chips.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sushi_pizza" title="Sushi pizza">Sushi pizza</a> is deep-fried rice or crab/imitation crab cake topped with mayonnaise and various sushi ingredients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Mexico_and_the_Western_United_States">Mexico and the Western United States</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Mexico and the Western United States"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sinaloan_sushi" title="Sinaloan sushi">Sinaloan sushi</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sinaloan_sushi" title="Sinaloan sushi">Sinaloan sushi</a> originated in <a href="/wiki/Sinaloa,_Mexico" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinaloa, Mexico">Sinaloa, Mexico</a> and has been available in the Western United States since 2013.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Similar_dishes_in_Asia">Similar dishes in Asia</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Similar dishes in Asia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="South_Korea">South Korea</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: South Korea"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Gimbap" title="Gimbap">Gimbap</a></i></span>, similar to <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makizushi</i></span>, is an internationally popular convenience food of Korean origin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Alexander&#95;111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It consists of <span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Gim_(food)" title="Gim (food)">gim</a></i></span> (the Korean version of nori) rolled around rice seasoned with sesame oil, instead of vinegar, and a variety of ingredients such as vegetables, like <span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Takuan" title="Takuan">danmuji</a></i></span>, and meat, like <span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Bulgogi" title="Bulgogi">bulgogi</a></i></span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Salon-Gimbap&#95;112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-Gimbap-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Ingredients">Ingredients</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ingredients"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm/250px--Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" height="140" data-durationhint="137" data-mwtitle="Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/82/Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm/Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-width="1080" data-height="606" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/82/Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm/Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm.144p.mjpeg.mov" type="video/quicktime" data-transcodekey="144p.mjpeg.mov" data-width="256" data-height="144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/82/Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm/Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/82/Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm/Making_Sushi.webmhd.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /></video></span><figcaption>Video of making sushi without fish</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sushichef.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sushichef.jpg/250px-Sushichef.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sushichef.jpg/500px-Sushichef.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a><figcaption>Sushi chef preparing <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">nigirizushi</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>, Japan</figcaption></figure>
<p>All sushi has a base of specially prepared rice, complemented with other ingredients. Traditional Japanese sushi consists of rice flavored with vinegar sauce and various raw or cooked ingredients.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="sushi&#95;rice"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sushi-meshi"><i>Sushi-meshi</i></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sushi-meshi"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Japonica_rice" title="Japonica rice">Japonica rice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice">Japanese rice</a></div>
<p><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Sushi-meshi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">鮨飯</span></span>; also known as <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">su-meshi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">酢飯</span></span>)</span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">shari</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">舎利</span></span>)</span>, or <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">gohan</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ご飯</span></span>)</span>)</span> is a preparation of white, short-grained, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice">Japanese rice</a> mixed with a dressing consisting of rice vinegar, sugar, salt, and occasionally <a href="/wiki/Kombu" title="Kombu">kombu</a> and sake. It must be cooled to room temperature before being used for a sushi filling, or it will get too sticky while seasoned. Traditionally, it is mixed with a <a href="/wiki/Hangiri" title="Hangiri">hangiri</a> (a round, flat-bottom wooden tub or barrel) and a <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shamoji" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamoji">shamoji</a></i></span> (a wooden paddle).
</p><p>Sushi rice is prepared with short-grain Japanese rice, which has a consistency that differs from long-grain strains such as those from <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, Thailand, and Vietnam. The essential quality is its stickiness or glutinousness, although the type of rice used for sushi differs from <a href="/wiki/Glutinous_rice" title="Glutinous rice">glutinous rice</a>. Freshly harvested rice (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">shinmai</i></span>) typically contains too much water and requires extra time to drain the rice cooker after washing. In some <a href="/wiki/Fusion_cuisine" title="Fusion cuisine">fusion cuisine</a> restaurants, short-grain brown rice and <a href="/wiki/Wild_rice" title="Wild rice">wild rice</a> are also used.
</p><p>There are regional variations in sushi rice, and individual chefs have their methods. Most of the variations are in the rice vinegar dressing: the <a href="/wiki/Kant%C5%8D_region" title="Kantō region">Kantō region</a> version of the dressing commonly uses more salt; in <a href="/wiki/Kansai_region" title="Kansai region">Kansai region</a>, the dressing has more sugar.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Nori">Nori</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Nori"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nori" title="Nori">Nori</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Nori.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Nori.jpg/250px-Nori.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="198" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Nori.jpg/500px-Nori.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="812" /></a><figcaption>Sheets of nori</figcaption></figure>
<p>The dark green seaweed wrappers used in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makimono</i></span> are called nori<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">海苔</span></span>)</span>. Nori is a type of <a href="/wiki/Red_algae" title="Red algae">red algae</a>, typically in the family <a href="/wiki/Bangiaceae" title="Bangiaceae">Bangiaceae</a>, traditionally cultivated in the harbors of Japan. Many different foliose red algae species are <a href="/wiki/Seaweed_farming" title="Seaweed farming">cultivated</a> to be used as sushi wrappers, however <i><a href="/wiki/Pyropia_tenera" title="Pyropia tenera">Pyropia tenera</a></i> is the most commonly farmed. This species ranks among the top three most commonly cultivated seaweed species in China, Korea, and Japan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Originally, algae was scraped from dock pilings, rolled out into thin, edible sheets, and dried in the sun, similar to making <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1nh_tr%C3%A1ng" title="Bánh tráng">rice paper</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Today, the commercial product is farmed, processed, toasted, packaged, and sold in sheets.
</p><p>The size of a nori sheet influences the size of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makimono</i></span>. A full-size sheet produces <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">futomaki</i></span>, and a half produces <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">hosomaki</i></span> and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">temaki</i></span>. To produce <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">gunkan</i></span> and some other <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makimono</i></span>, an appropriately sized piece of nori is cut from a whole sheet.
</p><p>Nori by itself is an edible snack and is available with salt or flavored with teriyaki sauce. The flavored variety, however, tends to be of lesser quality and is not suitable for sushi.
</p><p>When making <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">fukusazushi</i></span>, a paper-thin omelet may replace a sheet of nori as the wrapping. The omelet is traditionally made on a rectangular omelet pan, known as a <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Makiyakinabe" title="Makiyakinabe">makiyakinabe</a></i></span>, and used to form the pouch for the rice and fillings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Gu"><i>Gu</i></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Gu"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_sushi_and_sashimi_ingredients" title="List of sushi and sashimi ingredients">List of sushi and sashimi ingredients</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Wagyu_sushi_01.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Wagyu_sushi_01.jpg/250px-Wagyu_sushi_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Wagyu_sushi_01.jpg/500px-Wagyu_sushi_01.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>Sushi made of meats other than fish (whether raw or cooked) is a variation often seen in Japan.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Whole-eel.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Whole-eel.jpg/250px-Whole-eel.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Whole-eel.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="240" /></a><figcaption><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Yaki anago-ippon-nigiri</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">焼きアナゴ一本握り</span></span>)</span>&#160;– a roasted and sweet-sauced whole <a href="/wiki/Conger_eel" class="mw-redirect" title="Conger eel">conger eel</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Fried-shrimp.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Fried-shrimp.jpg/250px-Fried-shrimp.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b0/Fried-shrimp.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="240" /></a><figcaption><span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Ebifurai-maki</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">エビフライ巻き</span></span>)</span>&#160;– fried-shrimp roll</figcaption></figure>
<p>The ingredients used inside sushi are called <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">gu</i></span> and are, typically, varieties of fish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For culinary, sanitary, and aesthetic reasons, the minimum quality and freshness of fish to be eaten raw must be superior to that of fish that is to be cooked. Sushi chefs are trained to recognize important attributes, including smell, color, firmness, and freedom from parasites that may go undetected in a commercial inspection. Commonly used fish are tuna (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">maguro, shiro-maguro</i></span>), yellowtail (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">hamachi</i></span>), <a href="/wiki/Eastern_nannygai" title="Eastern nannygai">snapper</a> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kurodai</i></span>), <a href="/wiki/Mackerel_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mackerel (food)">mackerel</a> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">saba</i></span>), and salmon (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">sake</i></span>). The most valued sushi ingredient is <a href="/wiki/Toro_(sushi)" title="Toro (sushi)"><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">toro</i></span></a>, the fatty cut of the fish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This comes in a variety of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">ōtoro</i></span> (often from the <a href="/wiki/Thunnus" title="Thunnus">bluefin</a> species of tuna) and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%ABtoro" class="mw-redirect" title="Chūtoro">chūtoro</a></i></span>, meaning "middle toro", implying that it is halfway into the fattiness between <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">toro</i></span> and the regular cut. <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Aburi</i></span> style refers to nigiri sushi, where the fish is partially grilled (topside) and partially raw. Most nigiri sushi will have completely raw toppings, called <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">neta</i></span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other seafoods such as squid (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">ika</i></span>), eel (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Anago" title="Anago">anago</a></i></span> and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Unagi" title="Unagi">unagi</a></i></span>), <a href="/wiki/Daggertooth_pike_conger" title="Daggertooth pike conger">pike conger</a> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">hamo</i></span>), octopus (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">tako</i></span>), shrimp (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">ebi</i></span> and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">amaebi</i></span>), <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clam</a> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">mirugai</i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">aoyagi</i></span> and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">akagai</i></span>), fish roe (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">ikura</i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Masago" class="mw-redirect" title="Masago">masago</a></i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kazunoko</i></span> and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">tobiko</i></span>), sea urchin (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">uni</i></span>), crab (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kani</i></span>), and various kinds of shellfish (abalone, prawn, scallop) are the most popular seafoods in sushi. Oysters are less common, as the taste is thought to not go well with the rice. <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kani kama</i></span>, or imitation crab stick, is commonly substituted for real crab, most notably in California rolls.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Pickled <a href="/wiki/Daikon" title="Daikon">daikon</a> radish (<span title="Georgian-language romanization"><i lang="ka-Latn">takuan</i></span>) in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">shinko maki</i></span>, pickled vegetables (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Tsukemono" title="Tsukemono">tsukemono</a></i></span>), fermented soybeans (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">nattō</i></span>) in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">nattō maki</i></span>, avocado, cucumber in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kappa maki</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Asparagus" title="Asparagus">asparagus</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yam_(vegetable)" title="Yam (vegetable)">yam</a>, pickled <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Ume" class="mw-redirect" title="Ume">ume</a></i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Umeboshi" title="Umeboshi">umeboshi</a></i></span>), <a href="/wiki/Kanpy%C5%8D_(food)" title="Kanpyō (food)">gourd</a> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kanpyō</i></span>), <a href="/wiki/Burdock" class="mw-redirect" title="Burdock">burdock</a> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">gobo</i></span>), and sweet corn (sometimes mixed with mayonnaise) are plant products used in sushi.
</p><p>Tofu, eggs (in the form of slightly sweet, layered omelette called <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">tamagoyaki</i></span>), and raw quail eggs (as a <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">gunkan-maki</i></span> topping) are also common.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Condiments">Condiments</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Condiments"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Sushi is commonly eaten with condiments. Sushi may be dipped in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dyu" class="mw-redirect" title="Shōyu">shōyu</a></i></span> (soy sauce), and is usually flavored with wasabi, a piquant paste made from the grated stem of the <i><a href="/wiki/Wasabi" title="Wasabi">Wasabia japonica</a></i> plant. <a href="/wiki/Kewpie_(mayonnaise)" title="Kewpie (mayonnaise)">Japanese-style mayonnaise</a> is a common condiment in Japan on salmon, pork, and other sushi cuts.
</p><p>The traditional grating tool for wasabi is a sharkskin grater or <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">samegawa oroshi</i></span>. An imitation wasabi (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">seiyo-wasabi</i></span>), made from <a href="/wiki/Horseradish" title="Horseradish">horseradish</a>, mustard powder, and green dye, is common. It is found at lower-end <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kaiten-zushi</i></span> restaurants, in bento box sushi, and at most restaurants outside Japan. If manufactured in Japan, it may be labelled "Japanese Horseradish".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The spicy compound in both true and imitation wasabi is <a href="/wiki/Allyl_isothiocyanate" title="Allyl isothiocyanate">allyl isothiocyanate</a>, which has well-known <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">anti-microbial</a> properties. However, true wasabi may contain some other antimicrobials as well.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Gari</i></span> (sweet, pickled ginger) is eaten in between sushi courses to both cleanse the palate and aid in digestion. In Japan, <a href="/wiki/Green_tea" title="Green tea">green tea</a> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">ocha</i></span>) is invariably served together with sushi. Better sushi restaurants often use a distinctive premium tea known as <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Mecha_tea" class="mw-redirect" title="Mecha tea">mecha</a></i></span>. In sushi vocabulary, green tea is known as <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">agari</i></span>.
</p><p>Sushi may be garnished with <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Burdock_root" class="mw-redirect" title="Burdock root">gobo</a></i></span>, grated <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">daikon</i></span>, thinly sliced vegetables, carrots, radishes, and cucumbers that have been shaped to look like flowers, real flowers, or seaweed salad.
</p><p>When closely arranged on a tray, different pieces are often separated by green strips called <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">baran</i></span> or <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">kiri-zasa</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">切り笹</span></span>)</span>. These dividers prevent the flavors of neighboring pieces of sushi from mixing and help to achieve an attractive presentation. Originally, these were cut leaves from the <i><a href="/wiki/Aspidistra_elatior" title="Aspidistra elatior">Aspidistra elatior</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">葉蘭</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">haran</i></span>)</span> and <i>Sasa veitchii</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">熊笹</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">kuma-zasa</i></span>)</span> plants, respectively. Using actual leaves had the added benefit of releasing antimicrobial <a href="/wiki/Phytoncide" title="Phytoncide">phytoncides</a> when cut, thereby extending the limited shelf life of the sushi.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Sushi bento boxes are a staple of Japanese supermarkets and convenience stores. As these stores began rising in prominence in the 1960s, the labor-intensive cut leaves were increasingly replaced with green plastic to lower costs. This coincided with the increased prevalence of refrigeration, which extended sushi's shelf life without the need for cut leaves. Today plastic strips are commonly used in sushi bento boxes and, to a lesser degree, in sushi presentations found in sushi bars and restaurants. In store-sold or to-go packages of sushi, the plastic leaf strips are often used to prevent the rolls from coming into early or unwanted contact with the ginger and wasabi included with the dish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Nutrition">Nutrition</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Nutrition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sushi_Bento_in_Aeon_Mall_BSD_City_Indonesia_1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Sushi_Bento_in_Aeon_Mall_BSD_City_Indonesia_1.jpg/250px-Sushi_Bento_in_Aeon_Mall_BSD_City_Indonesia_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="193" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Sushi_Bento_in_Aeon_Mall_BSD_City_Indonesia_1.jpg/500px-Sushi_Bento_in_Aeon_Mall_BSD_City_Indonesia_1.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3818" data-file-height="2940" /></a><figcaption>Sushi in shops are usually sold in plastic trays.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The main ingredients of traditional Japanese sushi—raw fish and rice—are naturally low in fat and high in <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> (from the rice), <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamins</a>, and minerals, as are <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">gari</i></span> (pickled ginger) and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">nori</i></span> (seaweed). Other vegetables wrapped in sushi may also provide additional nutrients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Health_risks">Health risks</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Health risks"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fish_diseases_and_parasites" title="Fish diseases and parasites">Fish diseases and parasites</a></div>
<p>Potential chemical and biological hazards in sushi include environmental contaminants, <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>, and toxins.
</p><p>Large marine <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predators</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Pacific_bluefin_tuna" title="Pacific bluefin tuna">bluefin</a>) can harbor high levels of <a href="/wiki/Methylmercury" title="Methylmercury">methylmercury</a>, one of many toxins of <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">marine pollution</a>. Frequent or significantly large consumption of methylmercury can lead to developmental defects when consumed by certain higher-risk groups, including women who are pregnant or may become pregnant, nursing mothers, and young children.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2021 study in <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, Spain reported that the estimated exposure to methylmercury in sushi consumption by adolescents exceeded the tolerable daily intake.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A 2011 article reported approximately 18 million people infected with fish-borne <a href="/wiki/Trematoda" title="Trematoda">flukes</a> worldwide.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zoonoses&#95;and&#95;Public&#95;Health&#95;127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zoonoses_and_Public_Health-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Such an infection can be dangerous for expecting mothers due to the health risks that medical interventions or treatment measures may pose on the developing fetus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zoonoses&#95;and&#95;Public&#95;Health&#95;127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zoonoses_and_Public_Health-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Parasitic infections can have a wide range of health impacts, including bowel obstruction, anemia, liver disease, and more.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zoonoses&#95;and&#95;Public&#95;Health&#95;127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zoonoses_and_Public_Health-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These illnesses' impact can pose health concerns for the expecting mother and baby.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zoonoses&#95;and&#95;Public&#95;Health&#95;127-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zoonoses_and_Public_Health-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Sashimi or other types of sushi containing raw fish present a risk of infection by three main types of <a href="/wiki/Parasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasite">parasites</a>:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Clonorchis_sinensis" title="Clonorchis sinensis">Clonorchis sinensis</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Trematoda" title="Trematoda">fluke</a> which can cause <a href="/wiki/Clonorchiasis" title="Clonorchiasis">clonorchiasis</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Anisakis" title="Anisakis">Anisakis</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">roundworm</a> which can cause <a href="/wiki/Anisakiasis#Anisakiasis" class="mw-redirect" title="Anisakiasis">anisakiasis</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Diphyllobothrium" title="Diphyllobothrium">Diphyllobothrium</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Cestoda" title="Cestoda">tapeworm</a> which can cause <a href="/wiki/Diphyllobothriasis" title="Diphyllobothriasis">diphyllobothriasis</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<p>For these reasons, EU regulations forbid using raw fish that had not previously been frozen. It must be frozen at temperatures below −20&#160;°C (−4&#160;°F) in all product parts for no less than 24 hours.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EU&#95;rules&#95;131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EU_rules-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fish for sushi may be <a href="/wiki/Flash_freezing" title="Flash freezing">flash frozen</a> on fishing boats and by suppliers to temperatures as low as −60&#160;°C (−76&#160;°F).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Super-freezing destroys parasites, and also prevents oxidation of the blood in tuna flesh that causes discoloration at temperatures above −20&#160;°C (−4&#160;°F).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Calls for stricter analysis and regulation of seafood include improved product description. A 2021 DNA study in Italy found 30%–40% of fish species in sushi incorrectly described.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some forms of sushi, notably those containing the <a href="/wiki/Fugu" title="Fugu">fugu pufferfish</a> and some kinds of shellfish, can cause severe poisoning if not prepared properly. Fugu consumption, in particular, can be fatal. Fugu pufferfish have a lethal dose of <a href="/wiki/Tetrodotoxin" title="Tetrodotoxin">tetrodotoxin</a> in their internal organs and, by law in many countries, must be prepared by a licensed fugu-chef who has passed the prefectural examination in Japan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Licensing involves a written test, a fish-identification test, and a practical test that involves preparing the fugu and separating out the poisonous organs; only about 35 percent of applicants pass.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Sustainable_sushi">Sustainable sushi</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Sustainable sushi"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_sushi" title="Sustainable sushi">Sustainable sushi</a></div>
<p>Sustainable sushi is made from fished or farmed sources that can be maintained or whose future production does not significantly jeopardize the ecosystems from which it is acquired.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Presentation">Presentation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Presentation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sushi_Kanagawa_Japan_(2013).JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sushi_Kanagawa_Japan_%282013%29.JPG/250px-Sushi_Kanagawa_Japan_%282013%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sushi_Kanagawa_Japan_%282013%29.JPG/500px-Sushi_Kanagawa_Japan_%282013%29.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="4512" data-file-height="3000" /></a><figcaption>Sushi served on a wooden platter at a sushi restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Kanagawa_Prefecture" title="Kanagawa Prefecture">Kanagawa Prefecture</a>, Japan</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sushi_in_Vienna.jpeg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Sushi_in_Vienna.jpeg/250px-Sushi_in_Vienna.jpeg" decoding="async" width="250" height="313" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Sushi_in_Vienna.jpeg/500px-Sushi_in_Vienna.jpeg 1.5x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="1440" /></a><figcaption>Sushi in restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, Austria</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%E9%AB%98%E7%B4%9A%E5%BA%97%E3%80%8E%E3%81%A1%E3%82%85%E3%81%86%E5%BF%83%E3%80%8F%E3%81%AE%E5%AF%BF%E5%8F%B8.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/%E9%AB%98%E7%B4%9A%E5%BA%97%E3%80%8E%E3%81%A1%E3%82%85%E3%81%86%E5%BF%83%E3%80%8F%E3%81%AE%E5%AF%BF%E5%8F%B8.jpg/250px-%E9%AB%98%E7%B4%9A%E5%BA%97%E3%80%8E%E3%81%A1%E3%82%85%E3%81%86%E5%BF%83%E3%80%8F%E3%81%AE%E5%AF%BF%E5%8F%B8.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="141" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/%E9%AB%98%E7%B4%9A%E5%BA%97%E3%80%8E%E3%81%A1%E3%82%85%E3%81%86%E5%BF%83%E3%80%8F%E3%81%AE%E5%AF%BF%E5%8F%B8.jpg/500px-%E9%AB%98%E7%B4%9A%E5%BA%97%E3%80%8E%E3%81%A1%E3%82%85%E3%81%86%E5%BF%83%E3%80%8F%E3%81%AE%E5%AF%BF%E5%8F%B8.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3054" data-file-height="1719" /></a><figcaption>Stone plates are used at high-end Japanese restaurants in <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Carai,_Ibaraki" title="Ōarai, Ibaraki">Ōarai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibaraki_Prefecture" title="Ibaraki Prefecture">Ibaraki</a>, Japan.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Traditionally, sushi is served on minimalist Japanese-style plates—often made of wood or lacquer—and arranged to emphasize clean lines, open space, and a sense of tranquility, reflecting the aesthetic principles of Japanese cuisine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Many sushi restaurants offer fixed-price sets selected by the chef from the catch of the day.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These are often graded as <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8D-chiku-bai" class="mw-redirect" title="Shō-chiku-bai">shō-chiku-bai</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">松竹梅</span></span>)</span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">shō/matsu</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">松</span></span>, pine)</span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">chiku/take</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">竹</span></span>, bamboo)</span>, and <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">bai/ume</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">梅</span></span>, plum)</span>, with <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">matsu</i></span> being the most expensive and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">ume</i></span> the least expensive.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sushi restaurants often have private booth dining, where guests are asked to remove their shoes and leave them outside the room;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> however, most sushi bars offer diners a casual experience with an open dining room concept.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Meet-_Sushi.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Meet-_Sushi.jpg/250px-Meet-_Sushi.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Meet-_Sushi.jpg/500px-Meet-_Sushi.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a><figcaption>Meat sushi</figcaption></figure>
<p>Sushi may also be served <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Conveyor_belt_sushi" title="Conveyor belt sushi">kaiten zushi</a></i></span> (sushi train) style, in which color-coded plates of sushi are placed on a conveyor belt from which diners pick as they please.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After finishing, the bill is tallied by counting how many plates of each color have been taken. Newer <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kaiten zushi</i></span> restaurants use <a href="/wiki/Barcode" title="Barcode">barcodes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Radio-frequency_identification" title="Radio-frequency identification">RFID</a> tags embedded in the dishes to manage the time elapsed since each item was prepared.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There is a practice called <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Nyotaimori" title="Nyotaimori">nyotaimori</a></i></span> which entails serving sushi on the naked body of a woman.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Glossary">Glossary</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Glossary"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some specialized or slang terms are used in the sushi culture. Most of these terms are used only in sushi bars.
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Agari</i></span>: <abbr style="font-size:85%" title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#8201;</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">Rise up</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>, refers to green tea. <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Ocha</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">お茶</span></span>)</span> in usual Japanese.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Gari</i></span>: Sweet, pickled and sliced ginger, or sushi ginger (gari). <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Shōga</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">生姜</span></span>)</span> in standard Japanese.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Gyoku</i></span>: "Jewel". Sweet, cube-shaped omelette. 卵焼, 玉子焼<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Tamagoyaki" title="Tamagoyaki">Tamagoyaki</a></i></span><span style="margin-left:.09em">)</span></span> in standard Japanese.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Murasaki</i></span>: "Violet" or "purple" (color). Soy sauce. <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Shōyu</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">醤油</span></span>)</span> in standard Japanese.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Neta</i></span>: Toppings on nigiri or fillings in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">makimono</i></span>. A reversal of the standard Japanese <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">tane</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">種</span></span>)</span>.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Oaiso</i></span>: "Compliment". Bill or check. <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Oaiso</i></span> may be used in not only sushi bars but also <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Izakaya" title="Izakaya">izakaya</a></i></span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Okanjō</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">お勘定</span></span>)</span> or <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">chekku</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">チェック</span></span>)</span> in standard Japanese.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Otemoto</i></span>: Chopsticks.7 <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Otemoto</i></span> means the nearest thing to the customer seated. <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Hashi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">箸</span></span>)</span> or <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">ohashi</i></span> in standard Japanese.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Sabi</i></span>: Contracted form of wasabi<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">山葵</span></span>)</span>, also known as Japanese horseradish.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Shari</i></span>: Vinegar rice or rice. It may originally be from the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <span title="Sanskrit-language romanization"><i lang="sa-Latn">zaali</i></span> (<span title="Sanskrit-language text"><span lang="sa">शालि</span></span>) meaning rice,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (December 2025)">original research?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> or <span title="Sanskrit-language romanization"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aar%C4%ABra" title="Śarīra">Śarīra</a></i></span>. <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Gohan</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ご飯</span></span>)</span> or <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">meshi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">飯</span></span>)</span> in standard Japanese.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Tsume</i></span>: Sweet thick sauce mainly made of soy sauce. <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Nitsume</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">煮詰め</span></span>)</span> in standard Japanese.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etiquette">Etiquette</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sushi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Etiquette"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Customs_and_etiquette_in_Japanese_dining" title="Customs and etiquette in Japanese dining">Customs and etiquette in Japanese dining</a></div>
<p>Unlike sashimi, which is almost always eaten with chopsticks, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">nigirizushi</i></span> is traditionally eaten with the fingers, even in formal settings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although it is commonly served on a small platter with a side dish for dipping, sushi can also be served in a bento, a box with small compartments that hold the various dishes of the meal.
</p><p>Soy sauce is the usual condiment, and sushi is normally served with a small sauce dish or a compartment in the bento. Traditional etiquette suggests that the sushi is turned over so that only the topping is dipped to flavor it; the rice—which has already been seasoned with rice wine vinegar, sugar, salt, mirin, and kombu—would otherwise absorb too much soy sauce and would fall apart.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-honey&#95;151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honey-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, the sushi chef will add an appropriate amount of wasabi to the sushi while preparing it, and the diner should not add more.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-honey&#95;151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honey-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, today, wasabi is more a matter of personal taste, and even restaurants in Japan may serve wasabi on the side for customers to use at their discretion, even when there is wasabi already in the dish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px">

ReadHowYouWant.com. p.&#160;xvii. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1458764140" title="Special:BookSources/978-1458764140"><bdi>978-1458764140</bdi></a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210617105707/https://books.google.com/books?id=0ut9nOwsFegC&amp;q=Edomae&amp;pg=PR17">Archived</a> from the original on June 17, 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">November 1,</span> 2020</span>. <q>A nugget of rice was seasoned with vinegar and topped by a sliver of seafood fresh from the bay that was only a few blocks away. That is why a synonym for nigiri sushi is Edomae sushi: Edomae is "in front of Edo," i.e., the bay.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Connoisseur%27s+Guide+to+Sushi&amp;rft.pages=xvii&amp;rft.pub=ReadHowYouWant.com&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1458764140&amp;rft.aulast=Lowry&amp;rft.aufirst=Dave&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D0ut9nOwsFegC%26q%3DEdomae%26pg%3DPR17&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASushi" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFMouritsen2009" class="citation book cs1">Mouritsen, Ole G. (2009). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=RJxuV-0eT4UC&amp;q=%22edomae%22&amp;pg=PA17"><i>Sushi: Food for the Eye, the Body and the Soul</i></a>. Springer. p.&#160;17. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1441906182" title="Special:BookSources/978-1441906182"><bdi>978-1441906182</bdi></a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210617105637/https://books.google.com/books?id=RJxuV-0eT4UC&amp;q=%22edomae%22&amp;pg=PA17">Archived</a> from the original on June 17, 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">November 1,</span> 2020</span>. <q>Edomae-zushi or nigiri-zushi? Nigiri-zushi is also known as Edomae-zushi. Edomae refers to the small bay in Edo in front of the old palace that stood on the same site as the present-day imperial precinct in Tokyo. Fresh fish and shellfish caught in the bay were used locally to make sushi, known as Edomae-zushi. It has, however, been many years since these waters have been a source of seafood. Now the expression Edomae-zushi is employed as a synonym for high-quality nigiri-zushi.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Sushi%3A+Food+for+the+Eye%2C+the+Body+and+the+Soul&amp;rft.pages=17&amp;rft.pub=Springer&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=978-1441906182&amp;rft.aulast=Mouritsen&amp;rft.aufirst=Ole+G.&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DRJxuV-0eT4UC%26q%3D%2522edomae%2522%26pg%3DPA17&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASushi" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite&#95;note-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-114">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFShimbo2000" class="citation book cs1">Shimbo, Hiroko (November 8, 2000). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=VWLTngEACAAJ"><i>The Japanese Kitchen: 250 Recipes in a Traditional Spirit</i></a>.
